                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Jahn%E2%88%92teller+Effect&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Jahn%E2%88%92Teller%20effect">Jahn−Teller effect</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Topotactic+Epitaxy&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_topotactic%20epitaxy">topotactic epitaxy</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=K2mn%5Bfe6%5D&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_K2Mn%5BFe%28CN%296%5D">K<sub>2</sub>Mn[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=In+Situ+XRD&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_in%20situ%20XRD">in situ XRD</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with an inexhaustible supply of raw materials are expected to replace lithium-ion batteries as next-generation electrochemical energy storage devices. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 1 to 9">(1−9)</a> Prussian blue analogues (PBAs) with a formula of A<sub><i>x</i></sub>M[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub><i>y</i></sub>·<i>z</i>H<sub>2</sub>O (0 ≤ <i>x</i> ≤ 2, <i>y</i> &lt; 1, A = alkaline metal, M = Mn, Fe, Co, and Ni) represent one of the most promising cathode materials for SIBs because of their double perovskite open frameworks facilitating Na<sup>+</sup> de/intercalation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 10 to 19">(10−19)</a> Considering the low redox potentials (Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> couple), high cost (Co), and electrochemical inertness (Ni), reasonably affordable Mn-based PBAs <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 20 to 22">(20−22)</a> hold great promise because they are able to deliver the highest theoretical capacity of 170 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a high average voltage (3.45 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), rivalling that of the commercial cathode in lithium-ion batteries. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 23 and 24">(23,24)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">However, most of the Mn-based cathodes suffer from huge structural deformation during cycling caused by Jahn–Teller (JT) distortion of Mn<sup>3+</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 25 to 31">(25−31)</a> As a result, the Mn<sup>3+</sup> on the surface is extremely unstable, accelerating the Mn dissolution from the cathode into a liquid electrolyte, leading to the loss of active materials and gradual decay of capacity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32,33)</a> In particular, the JT distortion is more severe in common Na<sub>2</sub>Mn[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] (denoted as NMF) because of the huge structural distortion from cubic (denoted as <i>cub</i>) NaMn<sup>II</sup>[Fe<sup>III</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>] to tetragonal (denoted as <i>tet</i>) Mn<sup>III</sup>[Fe<sup>III</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>]. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32,33)</a> Although several methods including phase structure optimization, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 33 and 34">(33,34)</a> metal atom substitution, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35 ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 35 to 37">(35−37)</a> and solid electrolyte <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> have been explored to restrain the JT distortion in NMF, none of these routes could maintain a long-term cycle stability while overcoming the voltage degradations.</div><div class="NLM_p">Surprisingly, K<sub>2</sub>Mn[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] (denoted as KMF) has shown a better cycle stability and voltage retention capability than NMF when used as the cathode of SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 38 to 41">(38−41)</a> However, the KMF presents a more complicated K<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> coinsertion/extraction mechanism; <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> its underlying structural evolution and effect on JT distortion remains unclear. In addition, KMF with smaller size (&lt;50 nm) is usually obtained by the rapid nucleation rate, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 42 and 43">(42,43)</a> and the nano size further aggravates the dissolution of Mn. In fact, the particle size of practical cathode materials should be 3–5 μm, whose lower specific surface area can effectively weaken the Mn dissolution. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44 ref45 ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44 ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 44 to 46">(44−46)</a> More importantly, cathode materials in single crystal can further provide high mechanical strength and structural stability, which improve the cycling performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> Some works have made efforts to increase the crystallinity or particle size of KMF, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39 ref42" aria-label="reference 39 and 42">(39,42)</a> however, the microsized KMF is not favorable for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion because of the long Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion length. On the other hand, the microsized KMF can be assembled by submicrometer units, which will potentially compensate for the slow Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics, without the sacrifice of structural stability.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we propose a topotactic epitaxy strategy that converts Mn<sub>3</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> cube precursor into KMF octahedra (∼600 nm). By tuning the kinetics of topotactic transformation, six octahedra are edge-to-edge assembled into an octahedral superstructure (OS). The concept of topotactic epitaxy self-assembly (TESA) has been applied. As the cathode of SIBs, the KMF-OS self-assembly structure not only improves the stability, but also reduces the contact area with the electrolyte to inhibit the dissolution of Mn. Furthermore, we demonstrate that a much weaker JT deformation occurs in KMF than that in NMF by using in situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) characterization. KMF partly transforms into NMF with Na<sup>+</sup> de/intercalation, and the existing KMF disrupts the long-range JT order of NMF, thereby suppressing the overall JT distortion. As expected, the KMF-OS electrode exhibits excellent long-term cycling stability with 80% capacity retention, rivalling that of most PBA cathodes in SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">After mixing the solution of Mn<sup>2+</sup> and K<sub>3</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>], cubic phase Mn<sub>3</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> (space group <i>Fm</i>3<i>m</i>) was preferentially formed because of its smaller <i>K</i><sub>sp</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>) showed that Mn<sub>3</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> compounds are uniform cubes with a diameter of ∼1 μm. The actual formula of M<sub>3</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> (M = Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, Cu, and Zn) is M[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2/3</sub>·[□Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>1/3</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48 ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48 ref49" aria-label="reference 48 and 49">(48,49)</a> in which 33.33% [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] structural sites are replaced by ligand and zeolitic H<sub>2</sub>O (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). Because of the numerous [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies, the remaining [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>3–</sup> and K<sup>+</sup> in the solution can be inserted into the unit cell of Mn<sub>3</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>). Meanwhile, Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>) spectroscopy and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>) showed that the [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>3–</sup> group are gradually reduced to [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4–</sup>. Time-dependent XRD verified that Mn<sub>3</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> completely converted into KMF after reaction for 16 h (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>), and the reaction processes can be described by the following equations:<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/asset/images/medium/nn1c07231_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i4"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/asset/images/medium/nn1c07231_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i5"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span>Because KMF and Mn<sub>3</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> share the same Mn–N–C–Fe framework, the phase transition from Mn<sub>3</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> to the KMF phase can be regarded as a topotactic transformation process <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 50 and 51">(50,51)</a> in which the crystal lattice of KMF inherited the crystal orientation of the Mn<sub>3</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> precursor (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>). Recent works also reported a similar phase reduction process from Co<sub>3</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> to K<sub>2</sub>Co[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52 ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52 ref53" aria-label="reference 52 and 53">(52,53)</a> XRD Rietveld refinement of KMF (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>) was conducted with <i>Fullprof</i> software, confirming its orthorhombic (denoted as <i>ort</i>) phase (space group <i>Pmn</i>2<sub>1</sub>) with the lattice parameters of <i>a</i> = 10.1275 Å, <i>b</i> = 7.2956 Å, <i>c</i> = 7.0289 Å, <i>α</i> = <i>β</i> = <i>γ</i> = 90°. SEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a1, b1, and c1, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>) revealed that 6 KMF octahedra (∼600 nm) were assembled into OS with highly uniform size (∼2 μm). The corresponding structural models (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a2, b2, and c2) from three directions exhibited an orderly assembly behavior through slight fusion with adjacent edges. As a result, six cavities were generated on the six {111} facets of KMF-OS. Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) characterization further confirmed its structural features (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a3, b3, and c3). Moreover, the obvious diffraction spots in the selected-area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a4, b4, and c4) indicated the single-crystal feature of KMF-OS, suggesting that the six smaller octahedra were edge-to-edge assembled. Energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) mappings (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>) clearly show the uniform distribution of K, Mn, Fe, C, and N elements in KMF-OS. According to the results of thermogravimetric analysis (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>) and inductively coupled plasma-atomic emission spectrometry (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>), the chemical formula of KMF-OS is K<sub>1.56</sub>Mn[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.92</sub>□<sub>0.08</sub>·0.61 H<sub>2</sub>O.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/asset/images/medium/nn1c07231_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/asset/images/large/nn1c07231_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic topotactic transformation from Mn[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2/3</sub>·[□Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>1/3</sub> to K<sub>2</sub>Mn[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>].</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/asset/images/large/nn1c07231_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.1c07231&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/asset/images/medium/nn1c07231_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/asset/images/large/nn1c07231_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a1, b1, c1) SEM images; (a2, b2, c2) schematic models; (a3, b3, c3) TEM images; (a4, b4, c4) SAED patterns of KMF-OS obtained from three different directions ((a4) [010], (b4) [011], and (c4) [111]). Scale bar = 500 nm.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/asset/images/large/nn1c07231_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.1c07231&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">We took intermediate products of different stages to understand the formation mechanism of KMF-OS (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S10–S14</a>). The eight corners of the Mn<sub>3</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> cube gradually disappeared (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>) and a truncated cube was formed (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>) within 0.5 h, accompanied by the decreased XRD peak intensity of Mn<sub>3</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a, b</a>). In the next 1.5 h, six small octahedra were symmetrically generated on the six face centers of the truncated cube, forming a core–satellite structure (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>). EDS mappings (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>) show that the K element was concentrated in the six small octahedra. As the reaction progressed to 10 h (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>), the six small octahedra grew and the core–satellite structure was transformed into an OS. In conjunction with reduced Mn<sub>3</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> and the enhanced KMF XRD peaks (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5c, d</a>), we can infer that six small KMF octahedra were epitaxially grown on an Mn<sub>3</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> cube and assembled into KMF-OS in the manner of TESA.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/asset/images/medium/nn1c07231_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/asset/images/large/nn1c07231_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) Schematic diagram of structural evolution and phase transition from Mn<sub>3</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> cube to KMF-OS. (b) Role of K<sub>2</sub>EDTA on the formation of KMF octahedron with {111} exposed facet. (c) Illustration of the three major steps involved in the topotactic epitaxy self-assembly process.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/asset/images/large/nn1c07231_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.1c07231&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">This TESA is different from the reported epitaxy process that the formed phase inherits the original morphological feature without crystal facet conversion, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52 ref53 ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52 ref53 ref54" aria-label="reference 52 to 54">(52−54)</a> in which six octahedra are generated on the six corners of octahedron or eight cubes are produce on the eight corners of the cube. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> In our case, the Mn<sub>3</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> cube with the {100} facet eventually evolved into a KMF octahedron with {111} facet, which was achieved by the chelating agent ethylenediaminetetraacetic acid dipotassium (K<sub>2</sub>EDTA). Considering the exposed Mn atoms on the {111} facet of KMF, K<sub>2</sub>EDTA will adsorb on the {111} facet to form a highly stable Mn-EDTA complex, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> thereby slowing down the growth rate of {111} facets and generate octahedra (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b). As the reaction continues, the Mn<sub>3</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> core gradually decomposes and acts as a reservoir to continuously supply Mn<sup>2+</sup> and [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>3–</sup>, finally producing KMF-OS (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">A proper amount of K<sub>2</sub>EDTA (0.0607 g) was necessary for the assembly of KMF octahedra. Without K<sub>2</sub>EDTA, only cubes can be obtained (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15a</a>), suggesting the important role of K<sub>2</sub>EDTA on the formation of {111} facet. As the amount of K<sub>2</sub>EDTA increased to 0.02 or 0.04 g (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15b, c</a>), insufficient K<sub>2</sub>EDTA will only produce a small portion of octahedra. If the amount of K<sub>2</sub>EDTA was increased to 0.08 g, excessive K<sub>2</sub>EDTA will make the topotactic transition too slow to self-assemble, and only produced monodisperse octahedra (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15d</a>). Moreover, tuning the kinetics of topotactic transition was also important for assembly. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="Equation 2">Equation 2</a> shows that increasing the concentration of K<sup>+</sup> will promote the reaction to move in the forward direction, thus the introduction of K<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> can also control the reaction rate. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a> In the absence of K<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>, too low K<sup>+</sup> concentration will make the topotactic transition extremely difficult, and the product keep pristine cubic structure (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16a</a>). A lower amount of K<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> (0.01 or 0.02 g) lead the assemblies imperfect with poor structural stability (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16b, c</a>), whereas the higher amount of K<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> (0.06 g) makes the assembly too fast to generate superstructures (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16d</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performances of KMF-OS were evaluated in Na half-cells in the voltage range of 2.0–4.2 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a displays the first three charge–discharge curves of the KMF-OS electrode at 25 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, and the first charge and discharge capacities are 93.3 and 96.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with an initial Coulombic efficiency of 97.0%. Because the larger size of KMF inhibited the K<sup>+</sup> diffusion, the KMF-OS cathode was fully activated at the second cycle and reached the highest capacity of 120 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a). In addition, KMF-OS displayed stable charge–discharge voltage profiles and reversible plateaus (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b), with 80% capacity retention (81 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) after 1500 cycles and 74.7% capacity retention after 2000 cycles (Coulombic efficiency ∼99%) at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c). Even at a high specific current of 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, a reversible capacity of 70.0 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was acquired for KMF-OS with 80% capacity retention after 1300 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d). Furthermore, KMF-OS delivered an excellent rate capability of 120, 106, 96, 84, 73, 56 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.025, 0.05, 0.1, 0.5, 1, and 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17</a>). The comprehensive performance of KMF-OS was even comparable to the reported Na-based PBAs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>).</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/asset/images/medium/nn1c07231_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/asset/images/large/nn1c07231_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical performance of KMF-OS. (a) First three charge–discharge curves at 25 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Charge–discharge curves in different cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Cycling capacity and corresponding Coulombic efficiency during 1500 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Capacities of the KMF-OS at different current densities from 0.025 to 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> and long-term cycling performance at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/asset/images/large/nn1c07231_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.1c07231&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The SEM image (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18</a>) of KMF-OS electrode after 100 cycles shows that the OS kept a good assembled structure, indicating that the superstructure has a strong structural stability. The corresponding cycle performance also illustrated that the KMF-OS electrode had better cycle stability than KMF monodisperse octahedron (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S19</a>). The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption curves (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S20</a>) showed that the specific surface areas (SSA) of the monodisperse structure and superstructure were 6.58 and 4.76 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>. Therefore, the reduced surface area of the superstructure decreased the contact area between the KMF cathode and the electrolyte, thereby inhibiting the transition metal in the KMF cathode from dissolving in the electrolyte. The chemical species formed on different cathode surfaces after the cycling tests were investigated by XPS. The C 1s (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a, b) and O 1s (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c, d) peaks exhibited the presence of C–O and C═O species, indicating the formation of ester (R–CO<sub>2</sub>–R, R<sub>3</sub>CO–R), carbonate salts (M<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>), alkyl carbonate (ROCOOM), and hydrocarbon (R–CH<sub>2</sub>–R). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref57" aria-label="reference 32 and 57">(32,57)</a> In addition, the peak at 531.0 eV in O 1s was attributed to transition-metal oxides (TM–O), which was produced by the dissolved TM cations in KMF cathode. The F 1s spectra (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e, f) exhibited transition-metal fluorides (TM–F) around 685.84 eV. The decomposition of fluoroethylene carbonate produces HF in the presence of trace impurities (such as H<sub>2</sub>O), which then chemically attack the KMF cathode and produces TM–F. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref58" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref58" aria-label="reference 32 and 58">(32,58)</a> The formation of TM–F and TM–O would lead to degradation of the KMF cathode and irreversible loss of active components, further deteriorating the electrochemical performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Thus, less TM–F and TM–O was generated in KMF-OS than in the KMF monodisperse octahedron, indicating that the suppressing the dissolution of TM cations from the KMF cathode can provide a superior cycling stability of KMF-OS.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/asset/images/medium/nn1c07231_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/asset/images/large/nn1c07231_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. XPS spectra of (a, c, e) discharged KMF monodisperse octahedron and (b, d, f) KMF-OS after 1000 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (a, b) C 1s, (c, d) O 1s, and (e, f) F 1s.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/asset/images/large/nn1c07231_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.1c07231&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Generally, K<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mn[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] has three phases including <i>ort</i> K<sub>2</sub>Mn<sup>II</sup>[Fe<sup>II</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>], <i>cub</i> KMn<sup>II</sup>[Fe<sup>III</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>], and <i>tet</i> Mn<sup>III</sup>[Fe<sup>III</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>] because of the different content of K<sup>+</sup>, and all the three phases share a classic NaCl framework (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S21</a>). Among them, the <i>cub</i> phase grows in the [100] direction, whereas the <i>ort</i> and <i>tet</i> phases grow in the [110] direction. The XRD peak at ∼17° is assigned to the lattice parameter of <i>a</i><sub><i>ort</i></sub>, <i>c</i><sub><i>cub</i></sub>, and <i>c</i><sub><i>tet</i></sub>; the peak ∼24° is attributed to the lattice parameter of <i>b</i><sub><i>ort</i></sub> and <i>a</i><sub><i>tet</i></sub>; and the peak at 25.5° corresponds to the lattice parameter of <i>b</i><sub><i>ort</i></sub>. To further understand the outstanding cycle performance of KMF-OS, we conducted in situ XRD to investigate the change in lattice parameter during the first two cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a, b, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S22</a>).</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/asset/images/medium/nn1c07231_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/asset/images/large/nn1c07231_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Intensity contour maps of KMF-OS collected by in situ XRD and corresponding galvanostatic charge–discharge voltage profiles of the first two cycles at 25 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Corresponding changes in lattice parameter of KMF-OS of the first two cycles. (c) Scheme of conversion from KMF to NMF. (d) Advantages of KMF for disrupting the long-range JT order of NMF.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/asset/images/large/nn1c07231_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.1c07231&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">During the first charge, the first K<sup>+</sup> was extracted at 3.65 V, as shown in cyclic voltammetry (CV) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S23</a>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> and the unit cell of <i>ort</i> K<sub>2</sub>Mn<sup>II</sup>[Fe<sup>II</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>] [10.1275 Å × 7.2956 Å × 7.0289 Å, that is, <i>a</i><sub><i>ort</i></sub> × <i>b</i><sub><i>ort</i></sub> × <i>c</i><sub><i>ort</i></sub>] slightly expended to <i>cub</i> KMn<sup>II</sup>[Fe<sup>III</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>][(10.5148 Å)<sup>3</sup>, that is, <i>c</i><sub><i>cub</i></sub><sup>3</sup>] (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b). This voltage was higher than that of the first Na<sup>+</sup> extraction from NMF (3.45 V), because of the higher binding energy between K<sup>+</sup> and the MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> framework than that with Na<sup>+</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref59" aria-label="reference 59">(59)</a> In this step, Fe<sup>2+</sup> (3d<sup><i>6</i></sup>) was oxidized to Fe<sup>3+</sup> (3d<sup><i>5</i></sup>) by losing one e<sup>–</sup>. The stable configuration transition will not cause JT distortion and thus ensures the cycle stability of KMF. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Upon charging to 3.80 V, the unit cell of <i>cub</i> KMn<sup>II</sup>[Fe<sup>III</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>] shrank to <i>tet</i> Mn<sup>III</sup>[Fe<sup>III</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>] [10.1549 Å × (7.1925 Å)<sup>2</sup>, that is, <i>c</i><sub><i>tet</i></sub> × (<i>a</i><sub><i>tet</i></sub><sup>2</sup>)] via the second K<sup>+</sup> extraction (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b). Generally, the standard <i>tet</i> phase should satisfy the ideal <i>c</i><sub><i>tet</i></sub><i>/a</i><sub><i>tet</i></sub> ratio = 1.41421 in the absence of JT distortion (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S21</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> However, because of the unstable electronic configuration of Mn<sup>3+</sup> (3d<sup>4</sup>), the unit cell of <i>tet</i> Mn<sup>III</sup>[Fe<sup>III</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>] would deform and deviate from the ideal ratio. The <i>c</i><sub><i>tet</i></sub><i>/a</i><sub><i>tet</i></sub> ratio was calculated as 10.1549 Å/7.1925 Å = 1.41187 and the corresponding deviation was (1.41421 – 1.41187)/1.41421 × 100% = 0.165%, which was much smaller than that in NMF. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> This slight deformation indicated that a moderate JT distortion occurred from KMn<sup>II</sup>[Fe<sup>III</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>] to Mn<sup>III</sup>[Fe<sup>III</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>]. We further characterized the valence state of Mn element during the first cycle of charge and discharge, which confirmed the mutual transformation of Mn<sup>2+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S24</a>). In fact, when KMF was used as the cathode of potassium-ion battery, it showed highly reversible phase transition and excellent cycle stability, which is also proves the suppressed JT distortion of KMF. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">In the subsequent first discharge, CV exhibited three peaks at 3.59, 3.44, and 3.26 V (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S23</a>), suggesting that the original K<sup>+</sup> and the Na<sup>+</sup> from the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte would coinsert to the <i>tet</i> Mn[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> Because of the lower reaction barrier of K<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup> would preferentially return to <i>tet</i> Mn[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] than Na<sup>+</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref59" aria-label="reference 59">(59)</a> As the cycling continues, the high concentration of Na<sup>+</sup> promoted more Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into pristine PBA to replace K<sup>+</sup>, making KMF phase partly converted into NMF phase, which confirmed by increasing NMF and decreasing KMF peaks in XRD pattern (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S25</a>). EDS results showed that the ratio of K/Na is 5.75, 3.42, 0.157, and 0.132 after second, 100th, 1000th, and 2000th cycle (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S25a–d</a>), respectively. It can be calculated that the content of KMF was gradually decreasing from 85.2 to 77.4%, 13.6 and 11.6%, which was in good agreement with the nonlinear fitting model (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S26e</a>). We further used energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy to analyze the element distribution of the KMF cathode after 100 cycles. The EDS mappings clearly exhibited the uniform distribution of Mn, Fe, Na, K, C, and N elements, suggesting the diffuse spatial distribution of KMF and NMF (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S27</a>). Moreover, the content of KMF was 69.1%, which was consistent with the nonlinear fitting (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S28</a>). Thus, when Mn<sup>2+</sup> transformed to Mn<sup>3+</sup>, <i>cub</i> KMn<sup>II</sup>[Fe<sup>III</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>] and <i>cub</i> NaMn<sup>II</sup>[Fe<sup>III</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>] will together convert to <i>tet</i> Mn<sup>III</sup>[Fe<sup>III</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>] until the cycling terminated (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c). We further calculated that the deviation in second was 0.140%, where the lattice parameter changes from <i>cub</i> [(10.4723 Å)<sup>3</sup>] to <i>tet</i> [10.2744 Å × (7.2753 Å)<sup>2</sup>]. In addition, the deviation in 100th and 101th cycles was 0.075%, in which the lattice parameter changed from <i>cub</i> [(10.5709 Å)<sup>3</sup>] to <i>tet</i> [10.3193 Å × (7.2934 Å)<sup>2</sup>] (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S29 and S30</a>). Because of the weaker JT distortion in KMF than in NMF, the KMF domain can act as a stabilizer to disrupt the long-range JT order of NMF, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> thereby suppressing the overall JT distortion (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>d). We further took out the charged K<sub>2</sub>Mn[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] cathode and reassembled it into a Na half cell to evaluate its cycling performance in the K<sup>+</sup>-free condition (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S31</a>). However, it displayed poor cycle stability, which was attributed to the severe JT distortion in Na<sub>2</sub>Mn[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]. Therefore, the “stabilizer” KMF phase combined with the self-assembly structure synergistically ensured the stability of the phase transition and excellent cycle stability.</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we explore a topotactic transformation route to produce KMF octahedra by using the Mn<sub>3</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> cube as precursor. The numerous [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacant sites in Mn<sub>3</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> promote the phase transition for the formation of KMF phase. The chelating agent K<sub>2</sub>EDTA not only can slow down the release rate of Mn<sup>2+</sup>, but also serve as structure directing agents to induce the formation of {111} facets. The KMF octahedra can assemble into an octahedral superstructure by tuning the kinetics of the topotactic transformation. When KMF serves as cathode in SIBs, the KMF partly transforms into NMF with Na<sup>+</sup> de/intercalation, and the existing KMF with weaker JT distortion can act as the stabilizing factor to disrupt the long-range JT order of NMF, thereby suppressing the overall JT distortion. Moreover, the self-assembly structure not only improves the structural stability but also decreases the contact area between the KMF cathode and the electrolyte, thereby suppressing the dissolution of transition metal cations from the KMF cathode into the electrolyte. As a result, the KMF superstructure exhibits highly reversible phase transition as well as excellent long-term cycling stability (1500 cycles) with 80% capacity retention at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. This work not only promotes creative synthetic methodologies for topotactic epitaxy but also provides a path for exploring the relationship between JT structural deformation and cycle stability.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Synthesis of Self-Assembled EDTA-K<sub>2</sub>Mn[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] Octahedral Superstructures</h3><div class="NLM_p last">In a typical synthetic procedure, 0.0735 g of manganese(II) acetate tetrahydrate (MnAc<sub>2</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O), 0.0607 g of ethylenediaminetetraacetic acid dipotassium salt dihydrate (K<sub>2</sub>EDTA·2H<sub>2</sub>O), 0.0300 g of potassium sulfate (K<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>), and 0.250 g of polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP, M.W. 40 000) were dissolved in 20.0 mL of deionized (DI) water to form solution A; 0.0990 g of potassium hexacyanoferrate(III) (K<sub>3</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>) was dissolved in 20.0 mL of DI water to form solution B. Solution B was then added into solution A in 10 s with constant stirring. After continuous stirring for 120 s, the obtained brown mixed solution was aged at room temperature (25 °C) for 16 h under atmospheric pressure. The resulting brown precipitate was collected by centrifuging and washed with DI water and ethanol several times and finally dried under a vacuum at 100 °C for 12 h.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) of the samples was characterized by Bruker D8 advance X-ray diffractometer with Cu Kα radiation (λ = 1.54056 Å). Field-emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM) images were recorded by JSM-7600F (Japan) with an accelerating voltage of 15 kV. Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images were recorded by JEOL JEM-2100F (Japan) with an accelerating voltage of 200 kV. High-angle annular dark-field images (HAADF) were recorded by Talos F200X (US) with an accelerating voltage of 200 kV. Fourier-transform infrared (FTIR) spectra were collected by Bruker FTIR spectrometers (UK) and Nicolet 6700 FTIR spectrometers (Thermo Scientific, US). Elemental analyses were carried out with Agilent inductively coupled plasma-atomic emission spectrometer (ICPOES730). Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) was carried out with a Netzsch STA 449 (Jupiter, Germany) under an Ar atmosphere with a temperature increasing rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup>. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurements were carried out on an Axis Ultra spectrometer (UK) under ultrahigh vacuum conditions with a standard Al Kα excitation source (1486.6 eV). The charging effect was corrected by adjusting the binding energy of the main C 1s peak to 284.6 eV. Specific surface areas were measured by using at least 0.1 g samples at −196 °C through BET nitrogen adsorption–desorption (NOVA 2200e, Quanthachrome, USA).</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Electrochemical Tests</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The working electrode was made by mixing the active materials (70 wt %), Ketjen black (20 wt %), and polyvinylidenedifluoride (PVDF, 10 wt %) thoroughly and then dispersing in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP). Subsequently, the slurry was uniformly dispersed on an Al foil with an active material mass ∼2 mg cm<sup>–2</sup> and dried under vacuum at 120 °C for 12 h. The electrochemical performance was assessed using CR2032-type coin cells assembled in a glovebox filled with Ar (O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O content is less than 1 ppm). Na was employed as the counter electrode, and a glass microfiber filter (GF/D, Whatman) was acted as the separator. The electrolyte was 1.0 M sodium hexafluorophosphate (NaPF<sub>6</sub>) in ethylene carbonate/propylene carbonate (EC/DEC, 1:1 by volume), and 5% (by volume) of fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC). Galvanostatic charge–discharge performances were recorded by battery system (Neware, China) at room temperature in the voltage range of 2.0–4.2 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). In situ XRD measurements were carried out between 15 and 40° degrees in a stainless-steel Swagelok-type cell which was connected to a multichannel battery testing system (Neware, China).</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM images, XPS, TGA, and FTIR of KMF at different growth stages; Rietveld refined XRD and crystal structure of KMF; SEM images and ex situ XPS of KMF after cycling; BET data of KMF; in situ XRD curves, measured parameters from XRD and BET for KMF; comparison of the electrochemical performances with other PBA cathode in SIBs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.1c07231/suppl_file/nn1c07231_si_001.pdf">nn1c07231_si_001.pdf (2.79 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.1c07231" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1038/natrevmats.2018.13"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vaalma, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Buchholz, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Weil, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Passerini, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Cost and Resource Analysis of Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nat.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Selective Reduction Approach to Construct Robust Cu1.81S Truss Structures for High-Performance Sodium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Shang, Yang; Li, Xiaoxia; Huang, Shaozhuan; Chen, Song; Yang, Zhao; Guo, Lin; Yang, Hui Ying</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Matter</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">428-439</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">MATTCG</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2590-2385</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Inc.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The practical application of transition metal sulfides as anodes for sodium-ion batteries has long been hindered by the design and fabrication of stable electrodes.  Here, inspired by architecture, we design a stable electrode based on Cu1.81S micro-truss structures (MTs) for the first time.  The key to this "selective redn." strategy is to engrave Cu1.75S precursors with a truss structure by using an anti-redn. agent (Fe3+ ions), and a subsequent recrystn. process reinforces the truss structure.  The Cu1.81S MTs with high structural stability exhibits a pronounced stability enhancement for 1,000 cycles with 77.7% capacity retention and delivers excellent rate performance of 331 mAh g-1 at 3 A g-1.  As a new method for designing robust electrode structures, this synthetic strategy is not limited to the synthesis of truss structures.  It provides new insights into a new generation of electrode structures and could be applied to the structural design of other batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feixiang

                        cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Environmentally stable interface of layered oxide cathodes for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Guo Shaohua; Zhou Haoshen; Guo Shaohua; Li Qi; Zhou Haoshen; Liu Pan; Chen Mingwei; Liu Pan; Chen Mingwei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">135</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are strategically pivotal to achieving large-scale energy storage.  Layered oxides, especially manganese-based oxides, are the most popular cathodes due to their high reversible capacity and use of earth-abundant elements.  However, less noticed is the fact that the interface of layered cathodes always suffers from atmospheric and electrochemical corrosion, leading to severely diminished electrochemical properties.  Herein, we demonstrate an environmentally stable interface via the superficial concentration of titanium, which not only overcomes the above limitations, but also presents unique surface chemical/electrochemical properties.  The results show that the atomic-scale interface is composed of spinel-like titanium (III) oxides, enhancing the structural/electrochemical stability and electronic/ionic conductivity.  Consequently, the interface-engineered electrode shows excellent cycling performance among all layered manganese-based cathodes, as well as high-energy density.  Our findings highlight the significance of a stable interface and, moreover, open opportunities for the design of well-tailored cathode materials for sodium storage.The interface of layered cathodes for sodium ion batteries is subject to atmospheric and electrochemical corrosions.  Here, the authors demonstrate an environmentally stable interface via titanium enriched surface reconstruction in a layered manganese-based oxide.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2021.230324"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mei, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deng, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zou, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Reversible OP4 Phase in P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> Sodium Ion Cathode</span>. <i>J.     <span class="NLM_cas:volume">508</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">230324</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0378-7753</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">P2-Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 as a promising cathode material for sodium ion batteries (SIBs) has attracted much attention owing to high capacity and wide operation voltage.  However, it suffers from inferior rate and cycling performances due to P2-O2 phase transition and Na+/vacancy ordering during charge/discharge process.  Herein, a Fe/Ti co-substitution strategy is successfully utilized to mitigate these issues.  Notably, as probed by In-situ X-ray diffraction, the substitution of Ni with Fe significantly stabilizes the interlayer framework by impeding slab glide during deep Na+ extn., resulting in reversible P2-OP4 phase transition with smaller vol. change of host structure compared to P2-O2 transition.  Meanwhile, synchrotron X-ray diffraction shows that, the Na+/vacancy ordering is greatly suppressed by further replacing Mn with Ti, which breaks down the long-range ordering of Ni/Mn distribution, promoting Na ion transport.  In addn., the charge compensation mechanism of Fe/Ti co-substituted P2-Na0.67Ni0.23Fe0.1Mn0.57Ti0.1O2 (NFMT) revealed by Ex-situ X-ray absorption spectroscopy demonstrates that the incorporation of Fe3+/4+ redox couple largely improve the working voltage.  Consequently, the obtained NFMT cathode delivers a high energy d. over 420 Wh/kg within 4.3-2.6 V, accompanied with remarkably enhanced kinetics and capacity retention.  Such co-substitution strategy provides a promising approach to develop high-voltage and cycle-stable cathode materials for SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1002/smm2.1007"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kong, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, H.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, J.-Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Q.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Advanced Energy Materials for Flexible Batteries in Energy Storage: A Review</span>. <i>SmartMat</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>1</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">e1007</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Cell Press</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Prussian blue analogs have significant promise as active materials for the next generation of battery electrodes with improved cycle life and rate capability.  Their useful electrochem. properties include two independent redox centers per unit cell; a nanoporous, open framework for rapid ion conduction; high stability during ion (de)insertion; and structural and electrochem. tunability for diverse applications.  Here we share insights into how control of the five main crystallog. features (two transition-metal ions, the inserting ion, defects, and water) imparts control over the ion-insertion reaction.  We then identify five key opportunities to expand our understanding of these materials, including the role of water in their ion conduction, modeling, synthesis methods, use as anode materials, and technoeconomics.  Further research in these areas will accelerate the development of new, high-performing battery electrodes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref11" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201706825"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nai, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lou, X.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Full open-framework batteries for stationary energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pasta Mauro; Wessells Colin D; Liu Nian; McDowell Matthew T; Huggins Robert A; Nelson Johanna; Toney Michael F; Cui Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>

        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">New types of energy storage are needed in conjunction with the deployment of renewable energy sources and their integration with the electrical grid.  We have recently introduced a family of cathodes involving the reversible insertion of cations into materials with the Prussian Blue open-framework crystal structure.  Here we report a newly developed manganese hexacyanomanganate open-framework anode that has the same crystal structure.  By combining it with the previously reported copper hexacyanoferrate cathode we demonstrate a safe, fast, inexpensive, long-cycle life aqueous electrolyte battery, which involves the insertion of sodium ions.  This high rate, high efficiency cell shows a 96.7% round trip energy efficiency when cycled at a 5C rate and an 84.2% energy efficiency at a 50C rate.  There is no measurable capacity loss after 1,000 deep-discharge cycles.  Bulk quantities of the electrode materials can be produced by a room temperature chemical synthesis from earth-abundant precursors.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref15" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms1563"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wessells, C. A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cui, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Copper Hexacyanoferrate Battery Electrodes with Long Cycle Life and High Power</span>. <i>Nat.                 10.1038/ncomms1563</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D2%26publication_year%3D2011%26pages%3D550%26journal%3DNat.%2BCommun.%26author%3DC.%2BD.%2BWessells%26author%3DR.%2BA.%2BHuggins%26author%3DY.%2BCui%26title%3DCopper%2BHexacyanoferrate%2BBattery%2BElectrodes%2Bwith%2BLong%2BCycle%2BLife%2Band%2BHigh%2BPower%26doi%3D10.1038%252Fncomms1563&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.1c07231&amp;doiOfLink=10.1038%2Fncomms1563&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Copper hexacyanoferrate battery electrodes with long cycle life and high power</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wessells Colin D; Huggins Robert A; Cui Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">550</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Short-term transients, including those related to wind and solar sources, present challenges to the electrical grid.  Stationary energy storage systems that can operate for many cycles, at high power, with high round-trip energy efficiency, and at low cost are required.  Existing energy storage technologies cannot satisfy these requirements.  Here we show that crystalline nanoparticles of copper hexacyanoferrate, which has an ultra-low strain open framework structure, can be operated as a battery electrode in inexpensive aqueous electrolytes.  After 40,000 deep discharge cycles at a 17 C rate, 83% of the original capacity of copper hexacyanoferrate is retained.  Even at a very high cycling rate of 83 C, two thirds of its maximum discharge capacity is observed.  At modest current densities, round-trip energy efficiencies of 99% can be achieved.  The low-cost, scalable, room-temperature co-precipitation synthesis and excellent electrode performance of copper hexacyanoferrate make it attractive for large-scale energy storage systems.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref16" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201702856"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, J.</span></span>;                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Highly Crystallized Na2CoFe(CN)6 with Suppressed Lattice Defects as Superior Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Xianyong; Wu, Chenghao; Wei, Congxiao; Hu, Ling; Qian, Jiangfeng; Cao, Yuliang; Ai, Xinping; Wang, Jiulin; Yang, Hanxi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Prussian blue and its analogs have received particular attention as superior cathodes for Na-ion batteries due to their potential 2-Na storage capacity (∼170 mA-h g-1) and low cost.  However, most of the Prussian blue compds. obtained from the conventional synthetic routes contain large amts. of Fe(CN)6 vacancies and coordinated H2O mols., which leads to the collapse of cyano-bridged framework and serious deterioration of their Na-storage ability.  Herein, the authors propose a facile citrate-assisted controlled crystn. method to obtain low-defect Prussian blue lattice with greatly improved Na-storage capacity and cycling stability.  As an example, the as-prepd. Na2CoFe(CN)6 nanocrystals demonstrate a reversible 2-Na storage reaction with a high specific capacity of 150 mA-h g-1 and a ∼ 90% capacity retention over 200 cycles, possibly serving as a low cost and high performance cathode for Na-ion batteries.  In particular, the synthetic strategy described in this work may be extended to other coordination-framework materials for a wide range of energy conversion and storage applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref18" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b06880"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shao, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qian, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ai, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Low Defect FeFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> Framework as Stable Host Material for High Performance Li-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Low cost and high performance Li-ion batteries have been extensively pursued for grid-scale energy storage applications, however, their development has been impeded for a long time due to the lack of qualified cathode materials with not only decent electrochem. performance but also resource abundance and low price.  In this paper, we report Prussian-blue type FeFe(CN)6 nanocrystals with well-controlled lattice defects and perfect nanocubic morphol., which can exhibit a high Li-storage capacity of 160 mAh/g, a strong rate performance at 24 C, and a superior cycle stability with 90% capacity retention over 300 cycles. Potassium-ion batteries (KIBs) have aroused widespread attention in these very active research applications due to their earth abundance and similar low redox potential compared to Li-ion batteries (LIBs).  It is crit. to develop electrode materials with large ion diffusion channels and robust structures for long cycling performance in KIBs.  Metal coordination materials, including metal-org. frameworks, Prussian blue, and Prussian blue analog, as well as their composites and derivs., are known as promising materials for high-performance KIBs due to their open frameworks, large interstitial voids, functionality and tailorability. In addn., the methods to enhance their K-ion storage properties are summarized and discussed, such as morphol. engineering, doping, as well as compositing with other materials.                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hexacyanoferrate-Type Prussian Blue Analogs: Principles and Advances Toward High-Performance Sodium and Potassium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhou, Aijun; Cheng, Weijie; Wang, Wei; Zhao, Qiang; Xie, Jian; Zhang, Wuxing; Gao, Hongcai; Xue, Leigang; Li, Jingze</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Na-ion batteries (NIBs) and K-ion batteries (KIBs) are promising candidates for next-generation elec. energy storage applications due to their low costs and appreciable energy/power d. compared to Li-ion batteries.  In the search for viable electrode materials for NIBs and KIBs, Prussian blue analogs (PBAs) with inherent rigid and open frameworks and large interstitial voids have shown an impressive ability to accommodate big alkali-metal ions without structure collapse.  In particular, hexacyanoferrates (HCFs) utilizing abundant Fe(CN)6 resources are the most interesting subgroup of PBAs, being able to deliver a specific capacity of 70-170 mAh g-1 and a voltage of 2.5-3.8 V in NIBs/KIBs.  In this Review, a comprehensive discussion of the HCF-type cathode materials in terms of their structural features, redox mechanisms, synthesis control, and modification strategies based on research advances over the last ten years.  The methodologies and achievements in improving the material properties of HCFs including the compositional stoichiometry, crystal water, crystallinity, morphol., and elec. cond. are outlined, with the aim to promote understanding of these materials and provide new insights into future design of PBAs for advanced rechargeable batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref22" class="refNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit22" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201803609"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.-W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S.-L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-Abundance and Low-Cost Metal-Based Cathode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Problems, Progress, and Key Technologies</span>. <i>Adv.
